import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a pointwise 2D convolution operation.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv1d = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, stride=1, padding=0, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the pointwise 2D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, height, width).
        """
        return self.conv1d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 16
in_channels = 64
out_channels = 128
width = 1024
height = 1024

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels]

We need to replace a simple pointwise 2D convolution (1x1 kernel) with a custom CUDA kernel. Since it's 1x1 conv2d, it's essentially a matrix multiplication: each output pixel is weight @ input pixel + bias. That can be computed as a batched matrix multiplication or a grouped GEMM. In fact, a 1x1 conv2d is equivalent to a linear layer applied per spatial location: reshape input from (B, C, H, W) to (B*H*W, C_in), multiply by weight matrix (C_out, C_in) transposed, add bias, reshape back to (B, C_out, H, W). We can leverage cuBLAS or cutlass for this via `torch.matmul`. But we need a custom CUDA kernel using `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. Wait, PyTorch tensors are row-major (actually "strided", with last dimension contiguous). So weight `W` shape is (N, K) = (128, 64). The offset for (n,k) is `n*K + k`. That's row-major. For GEMM in column-major (cublas default): `C = alpha * op(A) * op(B) + beta * C`. If we set `op(A) = A` (no transpose), then A is col-major (lda = H*W, M, K). For B, we need `op(B) = transpose(B)` to get a KxN matrix from an NxK row-major matrix? Let's be careful. Let's write the full CUDA source string: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cublas_v2.h> #include <ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h> __global__ void add_bias_kernel(float* out, const float* bias, int N, int K, int S) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * K * S; if (idx < total) { int n = (idx / S) % K; // output channel out[idx] += bias[n]; } } void conv1x1_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, torch::Tensor output, int batch, int in_channels, int height, int width, int out_channels) { int S = height * width; float alpha = 1.0f; float beta = 0.0f; const float* A = input.data_ptr<float>(); const float* B = weight.data_ptr<float>(); float* C = output.data_ptr<float>(); cublasHandle_t handle = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDABlasHandle(); cublasStatus_t status = cublasSgemmStridedBatched( handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, S, out_channels, in_channels, &alpha, A, S, (long long)in_channels * S, B, in_channels, 0, &beta, C, S, (long long)out_channels * S, batch ); if (status != CUBLAS_STATUS_SUCCESS) { // handle error somehow; maybe throw AT_ERROR("cublasSgemmStridedBatched failed"); } if (bias.has_value()) { const float* bias_ptr = bias.value().data_ptr<float>(); int total = batch * out_channels * S; int threads = 256; int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; add_bias_kernel<<<blocks, threads, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( C, bias_ptr, batch, out_channels, S ); } } ``` Wait, there is a potential issue with `c10::optional<torch::Tensor>`. Let's consider if using a custom kernel could be faster due to better memory coalescing. In cuBLAS, the GEMM A matrix is col-major with lda=S. That's exactly our layout per batch. For each batch, cublas sees M=S=1M, K=64. This is a "tall-skinny" matrix. The access pattern for A: each column is contiguous (length S). Since K is small, cublas might partition the M dimension across blocks. Each block reads a tile of A (some rows, all columns). Since columns are contiguous, reading a tile of rows for a column involves non-contiguous access? Wait, in col-major, element (row, col) is at `col*lda + row`. So for a fixed column, rows are contiguous. If a block is assigned rows `m0..m1` and all columns, it reads for each column a contiguous chunk of rows. So memory access is contiguous and coalesced (if threads read adjacent rows). This is great for reading A. For B: B is `in_channels x out_channels` col-major, ldb=in_channels=64. For a fixed column (output channel), rows are contiguous (64 floats). Since 64 floats = 256 bytes, that's 4 or 8 cache lines. B is small and likely stays in L2/L1. For C: similar to A, writing columns (output channels) for contiguous rows. Thus, the GEMM is memory-optimal. It reads A once, B once (reused across rows), writes C once. Total HBM traffic: ~4GB (A) + small (B) + 2GB (C) = 6GB. At 3.35 TB/s, ~1.8 ms. Plus overhead. Wait, earlier I said total traffic is 12.8GB for naive dot product. Why the difference? Because in GEMM, B is small and read once (128*64*4 = 32KB), and A is read once. But in the naive per-pixel dot product, each thread reads its own set of weights, causing B to be read many times? Actually, total weight traffic is still the same if cached: each weight is used by many threads. In practice, weights are cached in L1/L2 and reused across the M dimension. So total effective HBM traffic is similar. The difference is that GEMM has optimized tiling to keep B in shared memory and reuse it across many A rows. The naive kernel would load weights per thread from HBM unless cached, but with 128 warps per SM, weight cache might not hold all. GEMM handles this by loading B into shared memory and reusing for a tile of A. So cuBLAS GEMM is indeed the right approach. I should check if PyTorch might already be using this GEMM path. So the kernel is: - Block: 128 threads. - Each thread picks one spatial position s. - Load all OC partial sums initialised to bias (if any) or 0. - Then for each input channel c: - Load input[b, c, s] once. - Load weights for all OC for channel c: weight[n, c] for n=0..OC-1. - Add to partial sums: sum[n] += input * weight[n][c]. - After loop, write all OC outputs for this s. But we can do: ``` for (int n = 0; n < out_channels; ++n) { float sum = bias ? bias[n] : 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < in_channels; ++c) sum += inputVal(c) * weight[n*IC + c]; output[n*M + s] = sum; } ``` This re-reads inputVal for each n, but we can load it into a register outside the n loop? For instance, the weight layout in PyTorch Conv2d is (out_channels, in_channels, 1, 1), but when we pass it to cublas, we treat it as (out_channels, in_channels) and use CUBLAS_OP_N. The input is B x C x H x W, stored row-major but in PyTorch, the outermost dimension is batch, then channel, then height, then width. So contiguous memory order is batch-major: [b][c][h][w]. For a fixed batch b, the next slice is channel c, then spatial HxW contiguous. To perform_convolution as matrix multiplication: reshape input to (B, C, H*W). For each batch item, we have matrix of size (C, S). Weight is (K, C) reshaped from (K, C, 1, 1). Output per batch item is (K, S). The operation is Y = W * X, where X is (C, S), W is (K, C). In column-major cublas, the matrices are stored column-major. To compute Y = W * X^T? Let's be careful. In standard `conv2d(1x1)`, output pixel (n, k, h, w) = sum_c input(n, c, h, w) * weight(k, c). So for fixed n, define x as size C x S where x[c, s] = input[n, c, s]. Define w as size K x C where w[k, c] = weight[k, c]. Then y[k, s] = sum_c w[k, c] * x[c, s]. If we store x in column-major with leading dimension = C? Wait contiguous memory layout for input[n, :, :, :] is a 3D tensor (C, H, W) with stride [H*W, W, 1]. Actually in PyTorch, contiguous memory for tensor of shape (B, C, H, W) means memory layout is B-major, C-major, H-major, W-minor. So for a fixed batch n, offset to channel c pixel (h,w) is (n*C + c)*H*W + h*W + w. So if we view the memory for a fixed batch as a matrix of shape (C, S), with row-major layout (C rows, S columns), the element at (c, s) is at offset c*S + s, where s = h*W + w. So the matrix X is in row-major format. cublas functions expect column-major by default. To compute Y = W * X where W is KxC, X is CxS, Y is KxS, in column-major terms: We could set A = X^T (S x C), op(A) = CUBLAS_OP_T; B = W^T (C x K), op(B) = CUBLAS_OP_T; then C = Y^T (S x K). But the permutation cost is 4 GiB read + 4 GiB write = 8 GiB of memory traffic. Original cublas strided batched without permutation: 4 GiB read A + 0.032 GiB read B + 8 GiB write C = 12 GiB. So permutation adds a lot of traffic. Not worth it. We interpreted B as column-major CxK. As we derived, B_mat(i_c, j_k) = memory[i_c + j_k * C] = weight[j_k, i_c] (since weight row-major KxC offset = j_k*C + i_c). So yes, B_mat is CxK column-major. ldb=C. This is correct. A is input data_ptr, lda=S. A_mat(i_s, j_c) = input[j_c, i_s] = memory[i_s + j_c * S]. Correct. C is output data_ptr, ldc=S. C_mat(i_s, j_k) = output[j_k, i_s] = memory[i_s + j_k * S]. Correct. So the math is C_mat = alpha * A_mat * B_mat. This yields the correct result mathematically. Let's examine the arithmetic again. B=16, C=64, K=128, S=1,048,576. Operation: Y = X * W^T where X is (B*S, C), W is (K, C). This is a GEMM of M=16,777,216, N=128, K=64. FLOPs: 2 * M * N * K = 2 * 16,777,216 * 128 * 64 = 274,877,906,944. Memory: read X: 16,777,216 * 64 * 4 = 4,294,967,296 bytes = 4 GiB. Read W: 128 * 64 * 4 = 32,768 bytes = 32 KiB. Write Y: 16,777,216 * 128 * 4 = 8,589,934,592 bytes = 8 GiB. Total DRAM: 12 GiB + small. Arithmetic intensity: 275 GFLOPs / 12 GiB = 22.9 FLOPs/byte. Peak FP32 TFLOPS: 67 TFLOPS. So compute roofline: min(12e9 / 3.35e12, 275e9 / 67e12) = min(3.58ms, 4.1ms) = 3.58ms. For tensor core TF32 (989 TFLOPS): compute bound is 0.28ms. Memory bound is 3.58ms. So using tensor cores is irrelevant unless we can reduce memory bandwidth, which we can't because it's a pure GEMM. So the op is memory/compute bound at ~3.5-4ms. To be faster, we need to be compute or memory efficient. Since it's roughly at the roofline, beating cuDNN by a lot is very hard unless cuDNN is suboptimal for this shape. The memory pattern is: for each channel c, threads in a warp access consecutive pixels (since pixel index is blockIdx.x*blockDim.x + threadIdx.x). Let's write a very simple custom kernel: each thread computes one output element. ```cpp __global__ void conv1x1_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int H, int W, int K) { int S = H * W; int total = B * K * S; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int n = idx / (K * S); int k = (idx / S) % K; int s = idx % S; float acc = 0.0f; int input_base = n * C * S + s; int weight_base = k * C; for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { acc += input[input_base + c * S] * weight[weight_base + c]; } if (bias != nullptr) { acc += bias[k]; } output[idx] = acc; } ``` This kernel computes each output pixel for each batch and channel. But optimized GEMM uses tiling: A is loaded into shared memory and reused across tiles of N. B is loaded into shared memory and reused across tiles of M. But memory bandwidth could be the bottleneck. Let's compute the arithmetic intensity. Each output pixel requires C loads from input (but cached), C loads from weight (reuse across spatial), 1 store. If we compute one full output channel at a time, we read weight (C floats) and all input spatial (B*H*W*C floats). Total reads = B*H*W*C + K*C + B*H*W*K (output). For B=16, H=W=1024, C=64, K=128: - Input: 16*64*1024*1024 = 1,073,741,824 floats = 4 GB - Weight: 128*64 = 8192 floats = 32 KB - Output: 16*128*1024*1024 = 2,147,483,648 floats = 8 GB - Total memory: ~12 GB. Compute: B*H*W*K*C*2 = 137,438,953,472 FLOPs ≈ 137 GFLOPs. - Arithmetic intensity = 137 GFLOPs / 12 GB = 11.4 FLOPs/byte. - Memory bandwidth of H100 = 3.35 TB/s. - Memory-bound time = 12 GB / 3.35 TB/s = 3.58 ms. - Compute-bound time at 67 TFLOPS = 137 / 67000 = 2.04 ms. The stride between spatial elements within a channel is 1, but between channels it's S. For a column-major matrix of size (B*S, C), element (row, col) is at row + col*(B*S). But actual offset is b*C*S + c*S + spatial_idx = row + c*S. Since row = b*S + spatial_idx. We need offset = row + c*LDA. If LDA = B*S, then offset = row + c*B*S. That's not our data layout. If LDA = S, then offset = row + c*S = b*S + spatial_idx + c*S. Actual offset is b*C*S + c*S + spatial_idx. These are not the same. So we can't directly merge B and S into a single GEMM without a transpose or copy. But we can do a batched GEMM where each batch item has M=S, K=C, N=K_out. That's what I did with `cublasSgemmStridedBatched`. PyTorch's own `addmm` for 3D tensors uses `bmm` or `baddbmm` which also uses cuBLAS' strided batched routines. So the computation path is essentially the same. Why doesn't it match cuDNN? Wait... Let me write the direct kernel: one thread per output pixel. ```cuda __global__ void conv1x1_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int H, int W, int K) { int total = B * K * H * W; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; for (int linear = idx; linear < total; linear += blockDim.x * gridDim.x) { int w = linear % W; int h = (linear / W) % H; int k = (linear / (H * W)) % K; int b = linear / (K * H * W); float acc = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float iv = input[((b * C + c) * H + h) * W + w]; float wv = weight[k * C + c]; acc += iv * wv; } if (bias != nullptr) acc += bias[k]; output[((b * K + k) * H + h) * W + w] = acc; } } ``` This accesses memory in a somewhat coalesced way? Let's see: thread 0 reads pixel (b=0,k=0,h=0,w=0), thread 1 reads (b=0,k=0,h=0,w=1). So they read consecutive input pixels: `input[... + 0]`, `input[... + 1]`. That's coalesced. Then they read the same weight for k=0: `weight[0*C + c]`. So weight access is broadcast. Good. When k changes, threads diverge in weight access. For k=0 vs k=1, they read different rows of weight. But within a warp, if threads have different k, weight access is not uniform. That could be bad. But if each block handles a range of linear indices where k is constant, then within a block, all threads read the same weight row. Actually, the linear index ordering is w, h, k, b. So consecutive threads have the same k until w and h overflow. For W=1024, H=1024, within a warp of 32 threads, all have the same k and b, and differ in w or h. So weight access is perfectly uniform within a warp! Great. Input access is coalesced (consecutive w). Output access is coalesced (consecutive w). This is a very good memory pattern.
